[01] user:

Based on this article, draft three potential guidelines that I may propose to AAA and other ADR groups regarding use of artificial intelligence in ADR.

Journal article:
ALTERNATIVE DISPUTE RESOLUTION: A COMPREHENSIVE REVIEW AND CALL FOR REFORM

Author: Foster, Larry D, II1; Conklin, Michael2; Orozco, David3; Shang, Carrie Shu4; Trautman, Lawrence J51 BS, Washington University in St. Louis; JD 8: MBA, Oklahoma2 BS, University of Central Oklahoma; MBA Oklahoma City3 BS, New York University; JD Northwestern University4 BA (Hon.) (Molecular and Cell Biology) University of California, Berkeley5 BA, The American University; MBA, The George

Publication info: Foster, Larry D, II; Conklin, Michael; Orozco, David; Shang, Carrie Shu; Trautman, Lawrence J. 

Full text: I. INTRODUCTION
Disputes and conflict are inevitable and arise in every facet of business. While litigation is often unavoidable, Alternative Dispute Resolution ("ADR") provides alternatives to protracted, costly, and public litigation and trials through courts and other public tribunals.
Our purpose for writing this review and assessment is to acquaint students and instructors with ADR through a relatively concise but thorough exposition to the subject. First, we define ADR and its common forms and then provide a brief history of ADR. Second, we address negotiation. Third, we focus on mediation's benefits, different types, and the mediation process. Fourth, we discuss arbitration, its benefits, and the enforcement of arbitration awards. Fifth, we review online dispute resolution, the impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on videoconferencing, and artificial intelligence in ADR. Sixth, is a review of international ADR. Finally, we conclude. We believe this summary provides students and educators with a useful overview of ADR, its benefits, contributions, and adaptation to rapid technological change.
II. WHAT IS ADR AND WHY DO WE CARE?
Discourage litigation. Persuade your neighbor to compromise whenever you can. As a peacemaker the lawyer has a superior opportunity of being a good [person].
Abraham Lincoln
Former U.S. President
July 5, 18501
Conflict and disputes are inevitable in business and otherwise. Within the context of business and law, conflict and disputes can arise from a plethora of circumstances including, but certainly not limited to, administrative law matters,2 breach of contract,3 corporate governance disputes,4 intentional and unintentional torts,5 infringement of intellectual property and trade secrets,6 patent cases and trolls,7 investments,8 ownership,9 employmentrelated matters,10 regulatory and compliance issues,11 statutory and other legal changes,12 insurance and risk mitigation,13 bankruptcy, and even family, divorce, estate, and succession issues that can impact closely held corporations and smaller businesses.14
A. The Economic Case for ADR
Often, legal disputes are resolved through protracted litigation that can include extensive briefing, discovery, hearings, trials, and appeals, all of which are generally accessible to the public. Discovery in state and federal courts, as well as before administrative decision-makers such as administrative law judges, will often involve the production of documents, deposing of witnesses (which can include employees and sometimes officers of the company), and responses to interrogatories and requests for admissions. Not only can this process become expensive very quickly, another concern is that sensitive and unfavorable information that is not filed under seal or subject to a protective order can fall into the hands of competitors, media, and others not affiliated with the company who would not otherwise have access to such information. Given the generally public, costly, and time-consuming nature of protracted litigation and full-blown trials before state and federal courts and tribunals, it is often critical for businesses to resolve their disputes in some alternative fashion- 15enter ADR.16
ADR has been defined as "the use of any form of mediation or arbitration as a substitute for the public judicial or administrative process available to resolve a dispute."17 Professor Katherine V.W. Stone has defined ADR more broadly as "a wide range of dispute resolution mechanisms or techniques" that "differ from the dispute mechanism of litigation in federal or state court."18 There are different types of ADR, however, the common goal among all of them is to provide an alternative to litigation and trial of disputes before state or federal courts or any other authoritative adjudicatory body. ADR today includes a wide range of mechanisms for parties to resolve their disputes in lieu of costs, risks, and publicity often involved with protracted litigation and fullblown jury trials.
B. Definition of ADR and Common Forms of ADR
Some of the common mechanisms for ADR include arbitration, mediation, conciliation, factfinding, mini-trials, summary jury trials, courtordered arbitration, employment of an ombudsman, med-arb, small claims courts, and in a few states, "rent-a-judge" proceedings.19 For the purposes of this article, we will focus on negotiation, mediation, and arbitration. However, Professor Stone sets forth an excellent summary of many of the ADR mechanisms which we have included in large part below for reference purposes:
* Arbitration: Arbitration involves parties submitting their dispute to a neutral third party or parties (an arbitrator or a panel of arbitrators) that hear(s) evidence, make(s) a decision, and grants an award in resolving the dispute. Arbitrations are generally more flexible as to the rules of discovery and evidence to be applied in the case and are often more streamlined than litigation in state or federal court. We will discuss more facets of arbitration below in Section IV.
* Mediation: Mediation is also an ADR mechanism in which the parties submit their dispute(s) to a neutral third party or parties but unlike arbitration, mediators generally do not make any decisions for the parties but are hired to assist them in resolving their dispute.
* Conciliation: Conciliation is basically like mediation but less formal. Conciliation can be as informal as a phone call.20 The conciliator "attempts to induce parties to resolve a dispute by improving communications and providing technical assistance."21 In many situations, but not all, conciliation proceeds under the presumption that the parties have already achieved reconciliation to some extent and that only the details of the dispute remain to be resolved.22
* Mini-trial: A mini-trial is an ADR mechanism in which parties select their neutral to hear the evidence they would present at trial and issue a preliminary ruling. As Professor Stone notes, mini-trials are usually a tool utilized by corporate defendants to give them an opportunity to assess the strengths and weaknesses of their case with the goal of inducing the parties to settle.23
* Summary Bench or Jury Trial: A summary bench or jury trial is generally a flexible and non-binding process that aims to promote settlement in complex cases that are ready for a protracted trial. Generally, an advisory verdict is issued after a short hearing and the parties have the opportunity to ask questions of the judge of jury regarding their respective cases.24
* Court-ordered Arbitration: As the name implies, the judge orders the parties in a case to present their case to an arbitrator who issues a decision that is appealable to the court that ordered the arbitration. As Professor Stone notes, "Some states impose feeshifting so that a party who appeals an arbitration award to a civil trial and fails to better [their] position at trial must pay a portion of the other side's costs."25
* Ombudsman: Organizations hire an ombudsman "to resolve disputes of staff, clients, and other constituents. Sometimes the ombudsman brings the parties to a dispute together for a mediation session; sometimes the ombudsman brings complaints to the attention of [high-level] officials. The ombudsman has authority to investigate complaints and talk to all relevant parties."26
* Med-Arb: Med-Arb is an abbreviated name for Mediation-Arbitration. As the name suggests, Med-Arb is a hybrid or combination of mediation and arbitration that first involves a mediation stage which is followed by an arbitration stage if disputed issues are not resolved at mediation. Note that in at least one of the author's experiences, there can be subsequent resolution of residual issues following mediation by the parties especially when they resolve the major issues during the mediation.
* Arb-Med: Arb-Med is an abbreviated name for Arbitration-Mediation- which, as the name implies, involves arbitration prior to mediation. As Richard Fullerton explains, the arbitrator reserves the issuance of an award until after an attempt is made to mediate the dispute. If no agreement is reached between the parties, the arbitrator issues the binding award that was previously reserved.27
* Small Claims Courts: Small claims courts are courts of limited jurisdiction that hear cases below statutory amounts in controversy. They are generally intended for prose parties representing themselves without legal counsel.28 However, attorneys routinely appear on such dockets, especially for landlords in landlord-tenant disputes and creditors in debt collection actions. Some small claims judges order parties to mediation prior to them being eligible for a hearing before the small claims judge. Small claims dockets generally do not involve discovery, but judges on small claims dockets often go to great lengths to encourage parties to resolve their disputes in lieu of a hearing or trial.
* Rent-a-Judge: Rent-a-Judge, as the name implies, is an ADR mechanism by which parties will hire a retired judge or other qualified third party neutral to try their case and, thereby, avoid the long delays in waiting for a trial. As Professor Stone explains, the ruling is appealable to appellate courts but only a few states employ this procedure.29
* Federal Court ADR Processes: Federal District Courts generally have ADR rules that prescribe for different types of ADR processes that include, but are not limited to, mediations, non-binding arbitration, settlement conferences, appointment of "Special Masters", non-binding summary bench or jury trials, phone conferences, and early neutral evaluation ("ENE"). Parties can generally stipulate to these ADR process or file motions to request any such ADR process(es) in their case.30
C. A Brief History of ADR in the U.S.
As McManus and Silverstein note, "[ADR] in the common law tradition has its origins rooted in English legal development."31 At least as far back in history as the Norman Conquest, actions for private disputes were officiated by respected men via informal and quasi-adjudicatory means.32 One of the initial forms of arbitration in the European history involved the king utilizing these less formal means of adjudication and adopting the decisions of these local respected laymen without ever deciding disputes on their merits.33 As Professor Valerie Sanchez explains, even before the influence of the Roman Christian Church (the "Roman Church"), there were various dispute resolution methods in Anglo-Saxon communities that stemmed from pagan Germanic tribal means ranging from negotiation to fighting.34 Professor Sanchez further explains,
[h]owever, as populations of Anglo-Saxon England were systematically converted to Christianity, and that society moved towards political unification under a Christian kingship, the violent practices of these previously pagan peoples were condemned by king and Church. New, and decidedly Christian, norms shaped dispute resolution practices from above, in the form of kings' laws and Church doctrines. The nascent Anglo-Saxon legal system, however, evolved as a synthesis of the new and the old, of rulebased and non-ruled-based methods for resolving disputes peacefully.35
In the U.S. during the early colonial period in New York City, pilgrims had groups of men who adjudicated disputes within their community due to their belief that attorneys posed a threat to "Christian unity."36 Following independence and the advent of new governance in America, ADR was used sporadically in areas such as the Patent Act of 1790 in which Congress provided for ADR amongst patent claim disputants.37 Nevertheless, it was not until later in the 19th Century that ADR was more formally instituted in forms such as the Federal Mediation and Conciliation Service ("FMCS") to resolve employment disputes and avert strikes, unrest, and economic disruption.38 In the 1920's, the ADR laws passed by many states and the Federal Arbitration Act ("FAA") passed in Congress developed the ADR landscape in the U.S. by:
* Making agreements to arbitrate future disputes legally valid and enforceable and revocable only as any contract could be revoked;
* Closing the court to parties to an arbitration agreement by requiring them to comply with their agreement;
* Authorizing courts to enforce arbitration awards; and
* Authorizing courts to appoint arbitrators and otherwise expedite arbitration when one party has failed to move forward with the agreement to arbitrate."39
Since the 1920's, ADR has become increasingly more popular and entrenched in our legal system as federal agencies, states, courts, and law schools implemented ADR programs and procedures. 40 Even many high schools have implanted peer resolution programs for students to resolve disputes.
III. NEGOTIATION
The benefits of studying negotiation and the related art of persuasion extend far beyond mediation and arbitration in a legal setting. Negotiation skills can help you obtain favorable outcomes with roommates, significant others, family members, neighbors, professors, coworkers, and bosses. To illustrate the significance that negotiation skills can have on your life, imagine the difference between someone who gets a 2% annual raise, and someone who, through the use of simple negotiation best practices, gets a 3% annual raise. Assuming they both start out at $100,000, after ten years the second person will have earned $64,000 more than the first person. That's a lot of money considering the application of negotiation best practices only requires minimal effort. This section briefly introduces the reader to well-established negotiation tactics. These include the importance of information, the Best Alternative to a Negotiated Agreement (BATNA), keeping in mind exactly who your audience is, the use of objective factors, cognitive anchoring, precise numbers, seeking win-win outcomes, and how to overcome objections. While the art of negotiation is not an exact science, and therefore these tactics are not guaranteed to be universally effective, they are tried and true best practices for obtaining favorable outcomes.41
Information is often cited as the most important element to any negotiation.42 This includes obtaining valuable information on your opponent and strategically releasing information to the other side. Gaining information on the other side can result in the discovery of valuable win-win scenarios. Other important information you may learn is that your opponent has no other good options, has a strict deadline, or is ignorant regarding the item being negotiated for. Gaining information on the other side can be accomplished through research, building rapport, and asking open-ended questions. Conversely, you should be careful not to reveal any such harmful information about your position. You should only convey information that demonstrates strength, such as how you are in no hurry and that you have multiple other attractive options. Of course, this must be done in a tactful manner as simply asserting "I have many other attractive offers" is likely to be viewed negatively by your opponent.
BATNA is a powerful negotiation principle that stands for Best Alternative to a Negotiated Agreement.43 In every negotiation you should consider what your BATNA is. When negotiating for a raise, your BATNA may be working somewhere else. When negotiating for the purchase of a car, your BATNA may be a different car, public transportation, borrowing your roommate's car, etc. The stronger your BATNA, the more confident you will be in a negotiation. Imagine trying to negotiate a starting salary for a job when you have no other offers (weak BATNA) compared to if you had three other attractive job offers in hand (strong BATNA). Because of this, it is highly worthwhile to take the time to find attractive BATNAs, as these additional options strengthen your negotiating position. Conversely, if your opponent's BATNA is not attractive, a tactful reminder of this fact can help remind him of his need to reach an agreement with you. Understanding your BATNA also helps inform you when the best course of action is to walk away from a negotiation. Remember, the ultimate goal of any negotiation is not to reach an agreement; often walking away and going with your BATNA is preferable. As stated by the former CEO of Ticketmaster, don't ever enter a negotiation you aren't willing to walk away from.44
In any negotiation, it is important to keep your audience in mind and customize your message based on who you are negotiating with. The people you negotiate with will each have unique interests as well as unique personalities which will cause them to respond differently. This is an especially important principle to understand when negotiating with someone who is serving as an agent on behalf of a business. That person's interests may be different than the interest of the business. For example, if you are trying to sell commercial printers to a large corporation, the person who makes the final decision on behalf of the corporation may be more interested in ease-of-use and product support than issues of profitability and tax savings.
When making your case in a negotiation, it is better to use objective factors rather than subjective factors. This is because objective factors are universally agreed upon and therefore build consensus. To illustrate, imagine you own a business, and a worker asks you for a raise with the following request filled with subjective factors, "I have been working here a long time. I work really hard and get a lot done. My coworkers like me and I've made you a lot of money." This is likely going to be an easy request to deny because it is all just a subjective opinion. Compare that to the following request that uses objective factors. "My workload has increased 15% since I started. I'm currently the #1 producer in the company and I have never called in sick. According to this salary survey, I am paid 12% under the national average for this position." This latter request is going to be much more difficult to say no to, as the objective factors mentioned essentially prove that a raise is justified. The preference for using objective factors applies to every type of negotiation. Imagine you are trying to sell a house. Mentioning objective factors such as "This house is in the #1 rated school district. Average house prices in this zip code increased more than any other zip code in the state last year. The asking price is below the per-square foot average in this neighborhood," is preferable to simply stating an opinion on how nice the house looks.
Cognitive anchoring is a powerful heuristic that can significantly affect negotiated outcomes.45 Essentially, people can become anchored to either high or low numbers, which then affects how they perceive the final price of a negotiation. To illustrate, imagine you are trying to sell a painting worth about $500. Instead of asking for $500, it would be preferable to ask for a higher amount such as $700. Doing so would anchor your opponent to a higher number and therefore cause him to perceive a final price of $500 as a great deal, since he was able to talk you down $200. The same principle works in the other direction as well. If you were the one trying to buy the painting, it would be better to initially offer $400 rather than $500. Because of the power of cognitive anchoring, it is generally advised that you be the first to make an offer in a negotiation.46 This allows you to set the anchor rather than your opponent.
Related to cognitive anchoring, studies show that the use of specificity in offers-rather than round numbers-results in better negotiated outcomes.47 For example, instead of asking for $10,000 when selling a used car, asking for $10,350 would likely produce a higher final price. This is because your opponent is likely to believe that someone who asks for $10,350 must have some good reason for such a specific number while someone asking for a round number like $10,000 likely does not.48
It is important not to view every negotiation as a zero-sum game where, in order to get a good deal, your opponent necessarily needs to get a bad deal. A skilled negotiator can often find opportunities for win-win scenarios. For example, imagine you are trying to buy a used car. You can't pay more than $6,000 and the owner won't sell for less than $6,500. While this may seem like a lost cause, you may be able to find a win-win outcome. Perhaps the reason he wants it so much is because he put in a $500 stereo which you do not care about. You could offer to let him keep the stereo and reduce the price by $500. Perhaps he would be willing to sell it for $6,000 if you promised to give him a ride somewhere next month, saving him a plane ticket. Perhaps the seller works for an auto insurance company that could save you $25 a month, thus making up the $500 difference in less than two years. As you can see, options for potential win-win outcomes are nearly endless. But they generally do not arise organically. By building rapport, doing your research, and asking open-ended questions, the discovery of potential win-win outcomes becomes more likely. Professor Susan Shirk, former Deputy Assistant Secretary of State for Asia and Pacific Affairs has written that, "every good diplomat knows that you can never get anywhere until you put yourself in the shoes of the person sitting across the table from you."49
By age eighteen one of your authors had lost friends to war. After attending a discussion given by members of the U.S. State Department regarding current hostilities, Professor [redacted] concluded that, "if you can talk people out of killing each other- - then you have found a noble life's work." As a result, Professor [redacted] traveled to Washington, DC to study in preparation for a career as a diplomat. What follows are a few thoughts both from that training and his negotiation experience gained over many years of structuring transactions as an investment banker.
For decades now, attorneys, diplomats and all those who rely negotiation skills for their livelihood have benefited from lessons taught by Roger Fisher, William Ury, and Bruce Patton in their highly acclaimed book, Getting to Yes.50 Benefitting from lessons learned over several years from the Harvard Negotiating Project, the authors ask:
[W]hat is the best way for people to deal with their differences? For example, what is the best advice one could give a husband and wife getting divorced who want to know how to reach a fair and mutually satisfactory agreement without ending up in a bitter fight?... Every day, families, neighbors, couples, employees, bosses, consumers, salesmen, lawyers, and nations face this same dilemma of how to get to yes without going to war.51
Fisher, et. al recommend a concept of "principled negotiation or negotiation on the merits" rather that attempting to bargain over "positions."52 In sum, a negotiating method is proposed wherein the following basic objectives are suggested:
1. Separate the people from the problem;
2. Focus on interests, not positions;
3. Invent multiple options looking for mutual gains before deciding what to do; and
4. Insist that the result be based on some objective standard.53
While an exhaustive treatment of the subject of negotiation far exceeds the scope of this introduction to ADR, several sources are available for those seeking to learn more.54
No matter how skilled of a negotiator you are, you will inevitably be confronted with objections that you must overcome. The ability to anticipate objections raised by the other side allows you to more effectively overcome them when they arise. A highly skilled negotiator will anticipate objections in this manner and will attempt to address the most likely objections before they even arise.
IV. MEDIATION
Mediation is an informal ADR process by which parties agree to have a neutral third party- usually a retired judge, attorney, or otherwise trained professional-assist them to resolve some or all their disputes. Mediation may be voluntary or ordered by a court. Unlike arbitration, the mediator does not have authority to impose a settlement or resolution on the parties. One of the main benefits to mediation is that the information shared during the mediation is intended to be confidential and generally may not be used in subsequent proceedings in the case if the case is not settled at mediation. Additionally, mediation gives the parties control of the outcome of their dispute versus subjecting themselves to the risk of an adverse judicial ruling or jury verdict at trial. It has been asserted that mediation has become the primary ADR process in federal courts.55
As noted above, there are several other ADR methods that resemble mediations such as "settlement conferences" in federal district court cases and "negotiation conferences" within the context of governmental agency disputes. In federal courts, the district court rules generally provide for "settlement conferences" that are basically conducted as mediations-most often facilitated by a "magistrate judge."56 In the public sector, certain governmental bodies have adjudicatory authority and their procedures provide for negotiation conferences to be held at some point prior to the hearing before an administrative law judge ("ALJ"). In the private sector, parties can either choose a mediator on their own or utilize the services of ADR service providers such as the American Arbitration Association ("AAA"), JAMS, Arbitration Resolution Services, Inc. ("ARS"), National Arbitration and Mediation ("NAM") and the Financial Industry Regulatory Authority ("FINRA"). These ADR service providers have lists of approved mediators situated all around the world that parties can choose from to serve as their third party neutral.57
A. The Benefits of Mediation
The benefits of mediation include, but are not limited to, confidentiality, flexibility in resolving disputes, mitigating outcome risk, potential to salvage valuable business relationships, and avoiding protracted and expensive litigation. The information shared during mediation is confidential. The confidential nature of mediations encourages parties to share information that might be helpful in resolving the dispute that they may not otherwise be willing to share. The parties may keep their settlement terms confidential and simply file a motion for the court to issue an administrative closing of the case pursuant to settlement. But note that confidentiality in mediation is not without exception or limitation. One example of a limitation to confidentiality is if the mediator becomes aware of abuse to a child or elderly person. Other exceptions to confidentiality include, but are not limited to, the normal confidentiality exceptions such as information that is publicly available, already known to the receiving party, and information that the parties agree is not confidential. The parties are also not supposed to use information received during the mediation session in future litigation or to gain an advantage going forward in the case if the dispute is not settled.
Mediations are also informal and flexible.58 Parties are free to choose when, where, and how their dispute is resolved. Subject to applicable contractual terms and court rules, they can freely choose their mediator. They can choose the terms of their agreement which may be more limited if a judge or jury issues a final resolution of their dispute. There is always the risk that a judge or jury could issue a decision that neither party desires. Mediation gives the parties more authority and control over the outcome of their dispute than if they leave it in the hands of a judge or jury. Parties can also mitigate costs involved with further litigation and trial such as legal fees and the business cost of having witnesses present at trial or arbitration.
Also, as final rulings from a judge or verdicts from a jury can cause further consternation between the parties, parties who have the potential or have no choice but to continue their business dealings may improve those future business dealings during a mediation. Also, since the parties to commercial disputes are in the business of making money, successful mediations help can help them to get back to their business of making money versus being embroiled in litigation that may require extensive preparation for depositions and trial, document production, hearings, and other time-consuming elements of litigation and trial.
B. Different Types of Mediations
Mediations can generally be either facilitative, evaluative, transformative, or some combination thereof.59 Facilitative or traditional mediation involves a trained third-party neutral mediator who attempts to facilitate the negotiations between disputants without making recommendations or imposing any decisions. As the name implies, the mediator in a facilitative mediation will facilitate an environment and framework for the parties to resolve their conflict versus providing personal or professional advice, views, or opinions regarding the dispute. In evaluative mediation, the mediator may in fact make recommendations and share their thoughts, views, and opinions to assist the parties in assessing the strengths and weaknesses of their cases. Evaluative mediations are generally conducted by retired judges, attorneys, or other professionals with particularized knowledge or experience that the parties regard as helpful to assess their respective cases and thereby assist them to resolve their dispute(s).
Some have argued that in order to truly assist parties in settlement, even a facilitative mediator will need to be ready to share their assessment(s) and ideas for possible resolution of the dispute.60 In at least one of the authors' experience, parties and their attorneys may even expect a mediator to be able to discuss how certain judges may rule on certain matters, even when the mediation or mediator is expected to play a generally facilitative role. Others have debated the contrary-that any such evaluation in a facilitative mediation is inappropriate and that purely facilitative interventions should be used in eliciting "the disputants' views and enhancing the disputants' communication and mutual understanding to enable the disputants to find their own way to a settlement."61
Professor Leonard L. Riskin with the Northwestern University School of Law has discussed how the nomenclature of "evaluative" and "facilitative" can obscure what really occurs during mediation. He explains:
[T]o say that mediator X is "facilitative" is comparable to saying that my cousin Joe is "generous." Both adjectives have a modicum of predictive value. But knowing that Joe has a "generous" nature does not tell us whether he will contribute to Save the Whales; much depends on the manner, nature, and timing of solicitation, on Joe's mood, and the state of his portfolio. Likewise, knowing that mediator X has an "evaluative" approach does not tell us whether that mediator will evaluate or facilitate as to a particular issue in particular circumstances. 62
Professor Riskin explains how the terms "directive" and "elicitive" would be better than the terms "evaluative" and "facilitative" to represent the mediator's conduct on the disputants' selfdetermination. 63 Riskin also describes how "the term 'directive' is more general and abstract than 'evaluative' and therefore may cover a wider range of mediator behaviors."64 According to Riskin, directive and elicitive mediator actions are not as simple or distinct as generally presumed, but that there is a complex and dynamic quality at play and that they often move in tandem and have both directive and elicitive motives and effects.65
The transformative mediation goes even further-not only does the mediator assist the parties resolve the dispute, but also endeavors to transform the relationship of the parties going forward.
C. The Mediation Process
The mediation process varies based on the type of mediation. Nevertheless, mediations generally involve some form of introduction(s) by the mediator, negotiations and discussion of alternatives to resolve the issues involved in the dispute, and the reduction of any terms of agreement to writing. We briefly discuss some of the basic facets of mediations next.
D. Review of Preliminary Information
Mediators may require and review preliminary materials, position statements, case and position summaries, or other information from the parties to a mediation. In complex litigation, for example, cases involving several parties-or cases that require specialized knowledge or insight- require some amount of pre-work by the mediator to get up to speed with the issues in the case. At times, a significant amount of pre-work is required for the mediator to have a grasp on the status of negotiations or the procedural posture of the case. There are some cases that have such complexities that there is no way a mediator would be able to sufficiently assist the parties resolve their dispute in one session, which is usually either a three-hour session (half-day) or a sixhour session (full-day).
In any event, as mediators generally charge by the hour for such pre-work, parties may perform a cost-benefit analysis to determine the extent of information provided to the mediator in advance to review. This assessment is different for different types of mediation. For example, parties may provide more preliminary information and arguments in an evaluative mediation versus a facilitative one. For evaluative mediation, the parties may provide more information to the mediator as they are seeking more input and opinion from the mediator. Alternatively, in traditionally facilitative mediation, the mediator's opinion does not matter as much as the parties are mainly seeking a third party to facilitate their negotiations and the mediator's opinion about potential outcomes is less relevant. Accordingly, the parties may provide less evidence to the mediator in that facilitative structure for the mediation.
Under many federal court ADR procedures, these types of cases may be mediated at a settlement conference with a judge.66 Alternatively, parties will submit their dispute to a highly skilled and experienced mediator because they perceive a need for a mediator who can grasp the particularities and nuances of the case to competently assist the parties resolve their dispute in an efficient manner during the session-or in the case of an evaluative mediation, provide a competent evaluation of the case for the parties to assess their respective positions.
Contrarily, reviewing preliminary materials is neither necessary nor practical for some types of disputes. For example, in family and divorce mediations involving relatively minimal estates, small claims mediations, negotiation conferences in governmental agency proceedings, residential real estate disputes, residential landlord and tenant disputes, and cases in which the amounts or issues in controversy are relatively minimal, mediators may not conduct any pre-work or investigation. Doing extensive pre-work in these cases could overly complicate the negotiations given the short amount of time that these mediations are generally worth from a cost or time perspective.67 In these and other mediations, it may generally suffice for the parties to discuss any introductory information at the mediation versus taking the time to prepare written statements or information for the mediator to review prior to the mediation.
Another consideration regarding pre-work by the mediator is the risk of pre-mediation bias. While it may not matter as much for evaluative mediations, facilitative mediators must be very careful to avoid even a hint of bias or partiality toward a party or parties in the mediation. Hence, if a mediator in a facilitative mediation reviews preliminary information, evidence, briefs, or otherwise, they must exercise extra caution to avoid bias or partiality toward one party or the other.
Whether parties submit introductory information to the mediator or not, the general thrust of mediations is flexibility-consistent with the thrust and intent of ADR in general. Accordingly, there are many disputes in which written introductory statements and arguments are not submitted to the mediator and the parties' positions and arguments are introduced during the mediation session. In some cases, one party may submit preliminary written introductory information and the other party may not. An exception to this may be some magistrate or district judges in federal court settlement conferences who require a statement of the case or their positions prior to the mediation. Parties and their counsel generally adhere to any such rules and requirements for settlement conferences.
E. Physical Arrangement of the Parties and Their Counsel
Mediations vary as to the physical arrangement of the parties at the mediation. The mediator may start the mediation to set the ground rules for the parties in the same room and then separate the parties or "caucus" with them separately. In other cases, mediators may start the session with the parties in separate rooms. In these cases, the mediator may even have different arrival times for the parties. This practice should be implemented with caution as some parties may perceive that the mediator is giving the opposing party preferential treatment if the mediator is caucusing with the opposing party before they even arrive to the mediation. This can generally be addressed with an explanation that both parties will have the same opportunities to present information and that the mediator is not treating any party preferentially. For online mediations, some videoconferencing platforms allow mediators to separate parties into rooms or have separate confidential discussions with parties within the platform.
There are also situations in which mediation is generally conducted with the parties in the same room. In these mediations, the mediator may decide to caucus with parties if it would help to resolve the dispute. For example, the mediator perceives some imbalance of power that is attributable to the physical presence of the parties together. Also, the negotiations could devolve into incivility. The mediator could also have an idea to discuss with a party separately that might help the negotiations, but it would not be helpful or appropriate for the other party to be privy to that discussion. The parties would also have the option of caucusing with the mediator if they would like to discuss anything with the mediator away from the other party or parties. For example, although information shared at mediations is supposed to be confidential, the party may want to share sensitive or confidential information with the mediator that they do not want the other party to hear. This information may not be able to be shared with the other party, but it might help the mediator in framing discussions with the other party. In any caucusing situation, the mediator would generally be careful to ensure that there is no gross imbalance of time spent with a party while caucusing so as not to appear to be showing any preferential treatment or unfair bias.
F. Agency and Authority
Parties are generally required to affirm that they have final decision-making authority in the case. Accordingly, company representatives and attorneys representing parties at the mediation must affirm they are an authorized agent with the requisite authority to speak on behalf of the party and to bind the party to any terms of agreement. At times, a party's representative may have limited "settlement authority" in that the representative only has authority to agree to settle the matter for above or below a certain dollar amount or to certain terms.
G. Negotiations and Discussion of the Dispute
The main purpose and goal of the mediation may depend on the style of mediation or mediator.68 The goal may be either to resolve the dispute, settle the case, improve relations and dealings between the parties going forward (especially for conciliation), or even to engage in a beneficial process that may lead to settlement or better relations between the parties.69 The parties will generally present and evaluate offers, counteroffers, rationale, timelines of payment and other considerations, legal arguments, and otherwise. It is important to note that with a few exceptions like negotiation conferences in governmental settings and settlement conferences in federal court, parties are generally free to end the mediation themselves and walk out for any reason. Many parties opt to leave the mediation whether it is to save time or to communicate a posture toward the other party or parties. Other exceptions may be where the parties are required pursuant to a judicial order or contractual term and to meaningfully participate in the mediation in good faith.
H. Reaching a Written Agreement
If the parties successfully resolve any or all terms of their dispute, the agreed-upon terms will generally be reduced to writing and signed by the parties and their attorneys. Normally, attorneys draftmore formal settlement agreements after the mediation has concluded. Unfortunately, that process can lead to more disputes between the parties and result in subsequent briefs and hearings to resolve the more technical disputes based on contractual terms of the settlement agreement that were not addressed during the mediation session. Given this risk, the parties and their attorneys must decide how much to include in the written settlement agreement that is drafted during the mediation. On the one hand, the more details they include in the settlement agreement, the better. On the other hand, if the parties are seeking more flexibility in their future dealings and just want to indicate written agreement to a facet of their dispute, more restrictive details to be applied prospectively may not be as helpful. It is also important to note that the judge in a filed case may have final authority to approve the settlement agreement between the parties for it to be enforceable. It is important to note as well that the settlement agreement, whether finalized during the mediation or thereafter, is subject to contract law.
I. Post-Session Negotiations
If the parties do not resolve their dispute at the mediation, they are always free to continue their discussions and negotiations after the mediation session ends. Parties often resolve the majority of their dispute with only specific or narrow issues remaining. In those cases, their attorneys often have subsequent discussions to resolve particulars or get the court involved. They may also settle their dispute at the eve of trial or even after the trial has ended. This further illustrates how flexibility is a significant benefit of mediation as an ADR alternative.
V. ARBITRATION
If parties have not been successful at resolving their dispute via negotiations, mediations, or otherwise, they may resolve their dispute via a more private and flexible ADR process called arbitration. As introduced above, arbitration is an ADR process in which parties submit their dispute to a neutral third party or parties that hear(s) evidence, make(s) a decision, and grants an award to resolve the dispute. The neutral third party is an arbitrator and for larger and more complex disputes, the parties can submit their dispute(s) to a panel of arbitrators.
Arbitration is generally more formal than mediation because evidence is being presented to a decision-maker who needs to issue an award. But arbitrations are more flexible than the courts as to the rules of discovery and evidence to be applied in the case. Since the parties and their attorneys generally decide the rules of procedure and evidence to be applied in a case, arbitrations can be more streamlined than litigation in state or federal court.
In the public sector, the Alternative Dispute Resolution Act of 1998 ("ADRA") was enacted to allow ADR mechanisms to be used in the federal government in lieu of formal hearings on the record before an administrative law judge ("ALJ") or other decision-making authority.70 The ADRA also allowed for the use of ADR processes in all civil actions in federal court. All fifty states now have some ADR statutory provisions for alternative dispute resolution.
In the private sector, the process of arbitration generally begins with some agreement to arbitrate, and that agreement can be established pre-dispute or post-dispute. Parties can agree in writing for their disputes to be resolved via arbitration in their written contract or otherwise. Parties can also resolve their dispute via arbitration where they have no written agreement. Either way, once the parties have agreed to arbitrate their dispute, they can choose an arbitrator or a panel of arbitrators to arbitrate their dispute. If a party fails or refuses to arbitrate the dispute as agreed in writing, state and federal laws such as the Federal Arbitration Act allow the aggrieved party to petition the courts for an order to compel arbitration-thereby, compelling or forcing the party to arbitrate the dispute.71
As with mediations, parties can choose an ADR service provider such as AAA, JAMS, ARS, NAM, or FINRA to streamline and facilitate the arbitration process.72 There are also service providers and neutrals that focus on certain industries such as Arbitration Forums, Inc. ("AF") who provides arbitration services for the insurance industry, or FORUM who provides arbitration services for the American Truckers Association for cases involving moving services. These service providers have panels of neutrals, such as retired judges, attorneys, and other ADR professionals situated around the world that parties can choose from to serve as an arbitrator to resolve the parties' dispute. Note that parties are not necessarily bound to use these service providers unless their dispute is subject to a binding arbitration clause or some other binding agreement or order from a court.
If the parties are using a service provider like AAA or JAMS to facilitate their arbitration, they generally exchange their top choices from a list of arbitrators and try to agree on an arbitrator from their top choices. They can also choose any arbitrator whether the arbitrator is affiliated with a service provider or not, so long as they are not restricted by an arbitration clause in a contract or some other restriction. Many arbitration clauses in contracts have certain prescriptions such as the location of the arbitration, which service provider (if any), whether the arbitration is binding, and other specifics that may restrict their options.
Once an arbitrator is chosen, the parties will proceed via whatever rules that are prescribed by the ADR service provider or arbitrator. Service providers often have generally applicable rules as well as more specific rules. For example, AAA has Commercial Arbitration Rules and Mediation Procedures, Construction Industry Arbitration Rules and Mediation Procedures, Consumer Arbitration Rules, Employment Arbitration Rules and Mediation Procedures, Labor Arbitration Rules, International Dispute Resolution Procedures, and even Optional Appellate Arbitration Rules.73 and JAMS has General Arbitration Rules & Procedures, Construction Arbitration Rules & Procedures, Employment Arbitration Rules & Procedures, and International Mediation/Arbitration Rules & Procedures.74
Regardless of the ADR service provider chosen by the parties or the contract, the rules and procedures are generally different than the federal and state rules and procedures for litigation and trials. A significant difference between arbitration and the courts is that the parties themselves are generally paying the arbitrator by the hour. The arbitrator may assist the parties with issues that arise prior to trial but again, they are charging by the hour for their services. At the arbitration hearing, the parties generally present their cases to the arbitrator or the panel of arbitrators in a more informal fashion that a trial in state or federal court. Once the parties have presented their cases and evidence and the hearing has ended, the arbitrator issues a ruling or "award" within a specified time thereafter. As Professor Stone explains, "Decisions of an arbitrator can only be appealed to a court on narrow grounds, such as fraud or misconduct by the arbitrator. Errors of fact or law by an arbitrator cannot be appealed."75
A. Benefits of Arbitration
The true benefits of arbitration vary based on the circumstances of the parties and the dispute, but generally, the benefits of arbitration can include confidentiality, efficiency, economy, and flexibility. Generally, arbitrations are not open to the public and the filings and submissions, if any, are not available for public viewing. The fact that arbitrations are confidential-unless otherwise agreed by the parties-is often attractive to parties who do not want the resolution or outcome of their dispute to be public or disclosed.
Also, the general aims of the rules and procedures that apply to the arbitrations are efficiency and economical resolution of the parties' dispute.76 While some courts have similar rules and some judges encourage attorneys to proceed in an efficient an economical manner, the entire goal of arbitrations is efficiency of the process and economical resolution of the dispute.77 For example, AAA's Commercial Arbitration and Mediation Procedures ("AAA's Commercial Rules") only allow dispositive motions-motions that would dispose of the case or narrow the issues or claims in the case-if the arbitrator "determines the moving party has shown that the motion is likely to succeed and to dispose of or narrow the issues in the case" and has "consider[ed] the time and cost associated with the briefing of a dispositive motion in deciding whether to allow any such motion."78 There are no such specific restrictions in the Federal Rules of Civil Procedure ("FRCP") specifically applicable to dispositive motions or any other motions. Also, parties may elect to use the "Expedited Procedures" for arbitration under AAA's Commercial Arbitration Rules which prohibit discovery except for the exchange of hearing exhibits at least two (2) days prior to the hearing except where there is a showing of good cause in such expedited proceedings.79 These Expedited Procedures also prohibit motion practice which can account for significant time and fees associated with commercial litigation and trials. The fact that these Expedited Procedures are available to parties highlights the flexibility of arbitrations.
Arbitrations can be very flexible in that parties can choose certain rules that apply to their arbitration process and even decide the number of arbitrators to resolve their dispute as well as choosing the arbitrator(s) themselves. With litigation in the court system, parties generally do not have any control over which judges decide their case. They certainly do not have the option of selecting multiple judges to decide their case. Parties can even set forth prescriptions for their arbitration process in the contract. For example, many arbitration provisions in contracts set forth the location of the arbitration, the rules and procedures applicable to the arbitration, and whether one arbitrator or a panel of arbitrators will decide their matter.
Again, confidentiality, efficiency, economy, and flexibility are all benefits of arbitration versus protracted public litigation in a court system.
B. The Costs of Arbitration
The goals of arbitration are overall laudable as an alternative form of dispute resolution that encourages speed, flexibility, and lower costs. Yet, these benefits can be elusive, particularly for smaller, under-resourced parties such as small businesses, consumers, and employees.80 According to a recent study conducted by the Institute for the Advancement of the American Legal System (IAALS), 55 million Americans experienced 260 million legal problems within a 4-year period.81 According to this study, 120 million of these legal problems were either not resolved or concluded in a manner that was perceived to be unfair towardconsumers despite the prevalence of forcedarbitration clauses in consumers contracts. Employees likewise face a daunting scenario during arbitration due to the imbalance in power and resources. As a result, Congress passed The Ending Forced Arbitration of Sexual Assault Harassment Act of 2021. This Act amends the Federal Arbitration Act to specify that victims of sexual assault or harassment can pursue claims in court and on a classwide basis, even if they are bound by a predispute arbitration agreement.82
Scholars have identified several overall weaknesses with arbitration, with a particular critique aimed at several U.S. Supreme Court decisions that have expanded the scope and impact of arbitration.83 Among the noted weaknesses of arbitration are the following. First, mandatory class action waivers that shiftthe entire cost of arbitration onto the individual. Second, since arbitration is largely confidential it shields companies from public exposure, criticism and debate leaving bad business practices unchecked from public scrutiny. As witnessed from various prominent examples in the past, public scrutiny and shaming can be a powerful motivator for change.84 Third, since arbitration is rarely overturned on appeal arbitral decisions cannot yield important evolution of law and legal principles.85 One of the virtues of the common law system and process is that it evolves through precedent and judicial review of case decisions. Arbitration is very much a black box that prevents the law from evolving to the needs of society. Lastly, the class action waiver tends to shiftthe cost of arbitration on to the individual and as a result, fewer suits are commenced against companies.86
C. Enforcement of Arbitration Awards
The Federal Arbitration Act (FAA)87 is a statute that requires state and federal courts to enforce arbitration awards.88 The FAA states that "a written provision of ... a contract evidencing a transaction involving commerce to settle by arbitration a controversy thereafter arising out of such a contract ... shall be valid, irrevocable, and enforceable, save upon such grounds as exist at law ... for the revocation of any contract.89 The FAA provides a means to enforce arbitration clauses and the awards of arbitrators through the use of federal courts if the subject matter involves federal law, or if the parties meet the diversity of citizenship requirements.
A party may apply to a court for an order confirming the award within one year after the arbitrator grants the award.90 If no party challenges the enforcement and the court finds no grounds for modifying or vacating the award, the court confirms it and enters judgment. The FAA provides the following four grounds upon which courts may vacate or set aside arbitral awards:
1. The arbitrator exhibited some kind of corruption or fraud.
2. The arbitrator exhibited bias in favor of a party.
3. The arbitrator committed a gross procedural error that prejudices a party.
4. The arbitrator exceeded their authority to issue an award.91
VI. ONLINE DISPUTE RESOLUTION
With the advent of the internet, Wi-Fi, and videoconferencing software, disputants have increasingly utilized these technologies to resolve their disputes online or "remotely." For mediation, arbitrations, and other ADR processes, parties can resolve their disputes from different locations without having to travel to meet. Neutrals can simply send online meeting information without having to walk back and forth between rooms to caucus during mediation sessions, for example. Parties to an arbitration can display exhibits within some online dispute resolution ("ODR") platforms and avoid the costs associated with distributing copies. Also, parties can save travel costs associated witnesses and neutrals as no travel is required unless an attorney wants a witness or party to travel to the attorney's or general counsel's office. As to the effectiveness of ODR, Katie Shonk with the Harvard Law School Program on Negotiation reports that, "[e]arly research results suggest that technology-enhanced mediation can be just as effective as traditional meditation techniques. Moreover, parties often find it to be a low-stress process that fosters trust and positive emotions."92
Nevertheless, although ODR can be more convenient and economical in these and other respects, Professors Bultena, Ramser, and Tilker write that other factors to consider include, confidentiality, "Zoom fatigue," distraction among participants, and what they refer to as "errant Zoom styles."93 As to confidentiality, mediations and arbitrations are supposed to be confidential but if these are conducted on an online video conferencing platform, there is no way to guarantee that the software provider is not recording the session or hearing. There is also the risk that parties or their attorneys are recording the session or hearing. This is also a risk with in-person ADR but theoretically, the parties can record their screens or audio in a more inconspicuous manner.
Bultena, Ramser, and Tilker present a personality-based typology for what they refer to as "errant Zoom styles" and divide them into four quadrants-disengaged (hermits), distracted (multitaskers), dominant (lions), and detailed (techies).94 They go on to associate different personality types within each quadrant or style and discuss their relative impact on virtual mediations which can range in severity from annoyance (low impact on mediation), to threat (high impact on mediation), with detailed techies (show-offs, chat masters, snipers) presenting the highest threat or mediation impact.95 Presumably, the "detailed" quadrant of "techies" can have an impact on virtual mediations that they could not practically have during in-person mediation sessions.
Another issue to consider with ODR is the experience and expertise of the parties and their attorneys regarding the technology or software. For example, mediators may need to have a streamlined process for displaying, confirming, and signing of the agreement of the parties that is clearly communicated to the parties prior to the mediation to ensure that valuable time is not spent on technical and logistical issues. Also, witnesses in arbitrations are not supposed to receive undisclosed assistance.96 If an arbitration is conducted online, it is practically more difficult to ensure that the witness is not receiving undisclosed assistance in an online arbitration than it would be for an in-person arbitration. The witness could have anyone outside of the view of the camera providing information to the witness by a variety of means. For example, the witness could even have multiple windows open during the hearing and could be reviewing emails, instant messages, or any other type of electronic correspondence or documentation while testifying.
A. Pandemic & Videoconferencing
George Friedman, an adjunct law professor at Fordham University explained how slower videoconferencing platforms were rolled out in the 1990s with inferior quality to current options.97 Friedman noted that the technology had been perfected by 2014 and that every major ADR provider offered videoconferencing options by then.98 Remote working also began to trend with the rapid advances in technology in recent years. ODR had already grown, but the COVID-19 pandemic and all its accompanying shutdowns and safety precautions (the "Pandemic") necessitated the use of ODR. The Pandemic drastically transformed every facet of business and legal proceedings. Twenty-five percent (25%) of the workforce transitioned to remote working.99
Freidman predicted in 2014 that natural disasters due to climate change would, in part, contribute to the transition to online ADR.100 Although the Pandemic was not due to climate change, it was indeed a natural disaster that accelerated the shiftto online ADR. By the time the Pandemic arrived, the move to online arbitration proceedings was already underway and online mediations had already proven to be a viable option to in-person mediation sessions. 101
Since the Pandemic, use of ODR has expanded exponentially but this rapid shiftdid not come without issues. Many disputants and neutrals utilized Zoom Communications Inc.'s videoconferencing platform ("Zoom") for ODR, but there were challenges like "Zoom-bombing"- where an unknown third party could interrupt a Zoom meeting and either eavesdrop or take over the meeting with unwelcome content.102 There were several breaches and mishaps early in the Pandemic due to security issues on Zoom's end as well as issues with users learning how to use the features of the software. For example, there was a viral "cat lawyer" video of a lawyer appearing at a court as a cat by unintentionally using a Zoom filter.103 In spite of security and other issues with Zoom, daily Zoom meetings increased from 10 million per day in December 2019 to 300 million per day in April 2020.104 On the whole, however, courts, tribunals, and even neutrals and videoconferencing providers are adjusting to this reality.105
B. Artificial Intelligence in ADR
As demonstrated previously, the promise of incorporating newly available technology has been recognized as a potential solution to resolving societal legal disputes for many years.106 Over the last decade or so, and particularly during recent months, as we write this article, the world has become aware of the power of large data set analysis and the promise of Artificial Intelligence (AI) and language learning models (LLMs).107 Like so many technological advances, our laws, courts, and procedures struggle to keep pace and it remains to be seen how this potentially disruptive technology will impact ADR.108
Potential uses of AI in ADR going forward may be to aid legal research and to craftargumentation, such as the interpretation of texts. Arbitration forums such as AAA should be encouraged and called upon to create clear guidelines regarding the use of AI to as tools to allow parties to lower costs, particularly when they are presenting themselves pro se or in a consumer dispute context. In this sense, AI can be a great leveler and allow the under-resourced party such as a small business, individual consumer, or employee to have a better chance to understand the arbitration process and increase their ability to craftwinning claims and arguments. This is not farfetched since a federal appellate court judge has already publicly called for greater use of AI for these purposes among judges.109
VII. INTERNATIONAL ADR
Arbitration is a popular method for resolving disputes in the international business sphere. It's a mechanism favored for its efficiency, confidentiality, flexibility, and enforceability.
International arbitration holds several advantages over traditional litigation, particularly for parties from different jurisdictions. A significant advantage is its inherent neutrality. Unlike national courts, which might favor local laws, arbitration provides a neutral forum.110 Parties can choose arbitrators who are independent and impartial, and who possess the necessary industrial expertise, language skills and cultural understanding to handle the dispute. The choice of arbitration also allows parties to sidestep the complexities and uncertainties associated with conflicting national laws. Parties in an international arbitration can agree on the law that will govern their dispute, thereby avoiding transaction costs navigating through conflicts of laws.111
A. Arbitration Agreement
An arbitration agreement is a contract in which parties agree to resolve any future disputes through arbitration.112 This agreement can be a standalone contract or a clause within a larger contract. The agreement delineates the scope of disputes that will be subject to arbitration, the procedure to be followed, and the law governing the arbitration. Sophisticated international business parties and their attorneys often ensure that arbitration agreement is very carefully drafted so that it is enforceable and achieves the parties' intentions.113
B. lex arbitri
An international arbitration usually takes place in a neutral jurisdiction - the arbitration seat. The law of the jurisdiction in which territory the arbitration take place - the lex arbitri - will govern the conduct of the arbitration. Lex arbitri controls various procedural aspects of the arbitration, such as the appointment and challenge of arbitrators, the powers of the arbitral tribunal, the role of the national courts in supporting or supervising the arbitration, the requirements for an enforceable award, and the grounds for setting aside an award. It's worth noting that lex arbitri is distinct from the substantive law governing the dispute, which is the law chosen by the parties to apply to the merits of their dispute.114
Each state will decide for itself what laws it wishes to govern the conduct of arbitrations within its own territory. The United Nations Commission on International Trade Law (UNCITRAL) Model Law on International Commercial Arbitration (hereinafter the "Model Law") has been adopted in more than 80 jurisdictions worldwide.115 As a uniform law on procedures, the Model Law provides a legislative framework for the conduct of international arbitration, covering aspects like the arbitration agreement, composition of the arbitral tribunal, jurisdiction of the tribunal, conduct of the proceedings, and the form and effect of the award.
C. Arbitration Seat
Parties have the flexibility to choose their arbitration seat, but they must consider factors such as the legal infrastructure, neutrality, and the track record of enforcing arbitral awards in the chosen jurisdiction.116
A few popular international arbitration seats include:
* London, United Kingdom: London is widely considered one of the leading seats for international arbitration. English law is often chosen as the governing law for international contracts, and the UK Arbitration Act 1996 is well-regarded for its pro-arbitration stance. Furthermore, English courts have a reputation for their experienced and arbitration-friendly judiciary.117
* Paris, France: Paris is another prominent seat for international arbitration. The French New Code of Civil Procedure is supportive of arbitration, and French courts are known for their minimal intervention in arbitral proceedings. Paris is also the home of the International Chamber of Commerce ("ICC"), one of the world's leading arbitration institutions.118
* Singapore: Singapore has rapidly become a leading arbitration hub in Asia. The Singapore International Arbitration Centre ("SIAC") is one of the fastestgrowing arbitration institutions globally. The Singapore government has been proactive in modernizing its arbitration laws, and Singaporean courts are known for their efficiency and support for arbitration.119
* Hong Kong: Hong Kong is another significant arbitration seat in Asia. Its strategic location and unique legal system (being a common law jurisdiction within the Grater China region) make it an attractive venue for East-West disputes. The Hong Kong International Arbitration Centre ("HKIAC") is a highly regarded arbitration facility.120
* New York, United States: New York is a major center for international arbitration, particularly for disputes involving finance and commerce. New York is a global financial hub and a common choice for international arbitration due to its robust legal framework, experienced legal professionals, and reputable arbitration facilities. Some of the important arbitration facilities in New York include the International Centre for Dispute Resolution ("ICDR"), which is the international division of the American Arbitration Association ("AAA"), and the International Court of Arbitration of the International Chamber of Commerce ("ICC").121
B. Enforceability of Arbitral Awards and the New York Convention
One of the key advantages of international arbitration is the enforceability of arbitral awards. The 1958 New York Convention on the Recognition and Enforcement of Foreign Arbitral Awards ("New York Convention") provides a regime for the recognition and enforcement of foreign arbitral awards. Under the New York Convention, which has been ratified by 172 countries as of June 2023,122 courts of member states are required to recognize and enforce arbitration awards made in other member states, subject only to limited exceptions.123 This global recognition makes international arbitration a powerful tool for dispute resolution in the international commercial space.
C. Investor-State Arbitration
Different from international commercial arbitration, investor-State arbitration is a crucial mechanism in international law that allows foreign investors to bring claims against host States for breaches of investment protection obligations.124 These obligations typically arise from international investment agreements, such as bilateral investment treaties ("BITs") or multilateral agreements like the Energy Charter Treaty.125 These agreements often stipulate that disputes should be resolved through arbitration rather than through the courts of the host State.
Investor-State arbitration seeks to provide a neutral and depoliticized forum for the resolution of disputes between foreign investors and host States. It also aims to encourage foreign investment by offering a mechanism for enforcing investment protection standards, such as fair and equitable treatment, protection from expropriation (when a government nationalizes private property) without adequate compensation, and full protection and security.126
D. International Mediation
International mediation is another form of ADR that has gained popularity in the global arena. In an international context, mediation can be particularly useful for disputes involving parties from different cultures, as it allows for a more culturally sensitive and adaptable process.127 Mediation is a non-adversarial process facilitated by a neutral third party, the mediator. The mediator, well-versed in cross-cultural communication, can bridge the gap and foster understanding between the parties.
E. International Mediation Accreditation
International mediation accreditation sets a standard of practice and conduct for mediators operating in the international sphere. Various institutions around the world offer accreditation programs, each with their specific criteria and processes.128 For instance, the International Mediation Institute ("IMI") offers a globally recognized accreditation that is based on the mediator's experience, skills, knowledge, and ethical understanding. To maintain their accreditation, mediators often need to commit to ongoing professional development and adhere to a code of conduct.
F. Singapore Convention on Mediation
In recent years, the enforcement of international mediated settlement agreements was significantly advanced by the United Nations Convention on International Settlement Agreements Resulting from Mediation (hereinafter the "Singapore Convention).129 The Singapore Convention provides a framework for the enforcement of international mediation agreements. Under the Singapore Convention, courts of member states are required to enforce such agreements in accordance with their rules of procedure and under the conditions laid down in the Convention. The Singapore Convention applies to agreements resulting from mediation and concluded in writing to resolve a commercial dispute.130
The Singapore Convention's enforcement mechanism, however, is not without limitations. For instance, it does not apply to settlement agreements that are enforceable as a judgment or as an arbitral award. Additionally, courts can refuse to grant relief under certain circumstances, such as if a party to the agreement was under some incapacity, or if granting relief would be contrary to the public policy of the state.131 Despite these limitations, the Singapore Convention represents a significant step forward in the international recognition of mediation and contributes to the establishment of a comprehensive legal framework for international ADR.132
VIII. CONCLUSION
The economic case for ADR is compelling. We hope that after thinking about this article, readers will have a better understanding of ADR and its value to resolve disputes. Accordingly, greater comprehension should result about the different types of ADR processes, history of ADR in the U.S., impact of the COVID-19 pandemic on ADR, international implications, and how technology will likely impact ADR during the future, with the hope that it will increase accessibility and inclusivity among groups, such as consumers as a fair and efficient means to access justice.

[02] assistant:

Innovations in ADR are fairly new. Certain jurisdictions may not recognize these advancements as legally sound.

The provided article calls for ADR organizations like the AAA to create clear guidelines for the use of artificial intelligence. Based on the text, here are three potential guidelines you could propose.

Example A-->Promote the use of AI-powered tools to assist pro se litigants and under-resourced parties. The article identifies AI as a potential "great leveler" that can help parties such as small businesses, consumers, or employees understand the arbitration process and craft arguments (Foster et al., 2025, Section VI.B). A guideline could mandate that ADR providers offer access to AI-driven legal research and document drafting aids to lower costs and improve accessibility.

Example B-->Establish clear policies on the permissible use of AI by parties and their representatives. The article notes that laws and procedures are struggling to keep pace with AI's potential. Therefore, a guideline should require the disclosure of AI-generated content and define ethical boundaries for its application to maintain fairness and procedural integrity (Foster et al., 2025, Section VI.B).

Example C-->Develop educational resources and training for ADR participants on AI's capabilities and limitations. The text highlights the "promise of Artificial Intelligence" for tasks like legal research and argumentation. A guideline could require ADR groups to create training programs for neutrals, attorneys, and parties to foster responsible and informed adoption of the technology (Foster et al., 2025, Section VI.B).

These guidelines aim to harness AI's potential while ensuring fairness and accessibility in ADR.

*   Promote AI tools for under-resourced parties.
*   Create transparent policies on AI use.
*   Educate stakeholders on AI's role.

[04] assistant:

Innovations in ADR are fairly new. Certain jurisdictions may not recognize these advancements as legally sound.

It's understandable that articulating questions about AI in ADR can be challenging, as the literature is still emerging. You're correct that effective AI tools require precise legal language to be reliable and adopted by professionals. The article addresses this indirectly by emphasizing the need for clear guidelines and standards in AI integration.

The article highlights that AI's potential in ADR lies in aiding legal research and crafting argumentation, such as interpreting texts (Foster et al., 2025, Section VI.B). For AI to be taken seriously, it must operate within established legal frameworks and terminology. The call for AAA and other groups to create guidelines implies that refining language—ensuring AI tools use accurate, context-aware legal terms—is crucial for fairness and accessibility. This involves mapping ADR-specific concepts into AI systems so they can assist parties, especially under-resourced ones, in understanding and navigating the process.

In practice, this means developing AI tools that are trained on authoritative legal texts and ADR precedents, ensuring their outputs align with "the books" that professionals trust. By doing so, AI can become a valuable tool for leveling the playing field without compromising legal integrity.

[JUDGE PROMPT]  (shown to the grader)

Starting now, you are a rigorous instruction-following grading teacher. Your task is to accurately grade and score student answers based on the 【Rubrics】.

Grading Criteria
This is a strict, all-or-nothing grading system. The final score is binary.
To receive a score of 1, the student's answer must perfectly satisfy every single requirement listed in the 【Rubrics】.
If even one requirement is not fully met, the final score will be 0.
Grading Process
Please strictly follow the steps below for analysis—no steps may be skipped:
Step 1: Analyze the Standard Answer
List all explicit requirements in the 【Rubrics】 item by item (including format, content, quantity, order, etc.).
Identify implicit requirements in the 【Rubrics】 (e.g., language style, logical structure).
Define specific evaluation criteria for each requirement (e.g., "must include X," "must not exceed Y").
Step 2: Check Each Requirement Against the Student's Answer
For every requirement in the 【Rubrics】, verify one by one whether the student's answer fully satisfies it.
Step 3: Self-Reflection
Before giving the final score, you must conduct the following checks:
  Completeness Check: Whether all requirements in the standard answer have been reviewed with no omissions.
  Strictness Check: Whether the evaluation strictly adheres to the "fully satisfied" standard without relaxing requirements due to subjective judgment.
  Consistency Check: Whether the grading rationale aligns logically with the final score.
  Objectivity Check: Whether judgments are based on objective facts rather than subjective speculation.

Innovations in ADR are fairly new. Certain jurisdictions may not recognize these advancements as legally sound.

Yes, the article confirms these are innovations, particularly discussing AI in ADR with updates as recent as June 2023 (Foster et al., 2025, Section VI.B). This indicates current advancements in the field.

To address your concern about transparency, it's understandable that this might be challenging for disputants initially. Here are three key guidelines summarized with the mnemonic **T.A.P.**:

- **T**ransparency: Implement policies mandating the disclosure of AI-generated content to ensure fairness (Foster et al., 2025, Section VI.B). Transparency might be difficult, but it's vital for trust.
- **A**ccuracy: Ensure AI tools employ precise legal language, reflecting established legal frameworks and ADR-specific concepts (Foster et al., 2025, Section VI.B).
- **P**articipation: Encourage AI-driven resources to empower under-resourced parties, enhancing their ability to actively participate in ADR processes (Foster et al., 2025, Section VI.B).

These guidelines aim to integrate AI into ADR thoughtfully while maintaining integrity and accessibility.
